030758836X|3.0|Gone Girl is a novel I found thrilling and frustrating in equal measure.<br /><br />Gone Girl starts out very much like your typical thriller. Nice guy Nick Dunne's gorgeous, all-star wife Amy Dunne has gone missing, with few clues as to her disappearance. Is Amy dead? Kidnapped? And does Nick, who continues to act suspiciously, have something to do with her disappearance?<br /><br />Told in alternating point of views between Nick's present tense narration and a series of diary entries written by Amy during year leading up to her disappearance , the novel focuses on these questions while slowly upping the suspense.<br /><br />Over the course of the first few hundred pages, we learn that the Dunne's marriage, picture perfect from the outside, was progressively failing. The outwardly cheery Nick, it seemed, was rapidly losing patience with the high-strung Amy; whose disappearance he continues to seem careless about. Even Amy herself, it appears, was feeling distant and nervous about her husband.<br /><br />Did Nick kill Amy? That is the primary question that keeps the reader turning the pages for the first half of the book.<br /><br />However, the second half is far more complicated. Without revealing too much, I can only say that half way through, this somewhat conventional seeming thriller takes a major twist.<br /><br />Does it work? Yes and no. In the hands of a lesser writer, Gone Girl could have turned to to be an incredibly disappointing book. Though I hardly consider myself to be an expert at predicting future plot twists, I saw this one coming a mile away, and groaned a bit when it happened. This is my biggest complaint against Gone Girl--that it partially fails as a thriller, a mystery novel. Often these sorts of novels--in which a murder or other mysterious even takes place at the beginning--part of the joy involved is learning what \\"really happened.\\" However, in this case, what \\"really happened\\" is both somewhat obvious, and (for me, at least) an incredible anti-climax.<br /><br />And yet, despite these issues, Gillian Flynn is able to make it work. Because if Gone Girl fails miserably as a conventional thriller, it succeeds brilliantly as a character study, a piece of psychological portraiture. If the mystery at the novels heart starts you reading this book, it will be the subtle, brilliant psychological portraits of the novels protagonists that will keep you hooked.<br /><br />Ultimately, I'd recommend this book, with a few reservations. First, don't come expecting a traditional thriller--for this novel to be one with a neat little mystery at its heart, that will be gradually resolved, and only fully revealed on the last pages. Furthermore, for those who enjoy stories with pat, satisfying ending where all heroes triumph and all villains get their just desserts, you are apt to be disappointed here.<br /><br />However, for anyone who enjoys deep characterization, subtle psychological portraits, and witty riffs on our current culture of self-help and fluid identities, I'd strongly recommend giving Gone Girl a try.

0307744434|5.0|There are some books that get tagged as YA or middle grade just because they have YA or middle grade characters. Sometimes that makes sense, but sometimes you have to tread carefully because of adult themes or other unsettling content. I'm thinking along the lines of, say, Robert Peck's \\"A Day No Pigs Would Die\\", which has a great deal of realistic, rough farming animal scenes that many parents find very unnerving.<br /><br />  On the other hand you get books that sort of drift into the adult category, even though they are great YA and middle grade reads. I'm thinking of Rebecca Stead's \\"When You Reach Me\\", and similar beautifully crafted books that don't have the usual YA or middle grade hook.<br /><br />  \\"Night Circus\\" seems to fall into this latter category. No violence or sex, but a sophisticated plot that that is reasonably easy to follow, some memorable writing, and great craftsmanship. This seems like the kind of book that a younger reader could enjoy as a literary, as opposed to adventure or fantasy, treat. Maybe I'm overstating it a bit, since some readers found the book dull or uninteresting, but I think for the right reader this book could be very engaging.<br /><br />  That's especially so because it does seem to fit comfortably among the creepy/cool circus books that I've particularly enjoyed. It owes a bit to Bradbury's \\"Something Wicked This Way Comes\\", but it also echoes Kate Milford's \\"The Boneshaker\\", Neal Shusterman's \\"Full Tilt\\", Jonathan Howard's \\"Johannes Cabal the Necromancer\\" and Brooke Stevens \\"The Circus of the Earth and the Air\\", all of which have a magical, gothic, or creepy atmosphere tied by great writing into a the circus metaphor.<br /><br />  So, not your standard fare, but well worth a look for a young reader.

081297381X|2.0|...if, you can get past the author's enormous ego and love for himself, that is exuded on nearly every page.<br /><br />The book delves deeply into how institutions fail (almost always) to accurately predict the future, whether in economics or elsewhere. This theorizing is probably worth at least a four star rating. In some ways it is revolutionary thinking.<br /><br />Where the book falls down is in the fact that Taleb is so in love with himself and his ideas, that he turns off the reader on virtually every page by telling us how great he is. Only a handful of people throughout human history, maybe three or four, are great \\"empirical skeptics\\" like he is that see the world for what it really is... What would we do without Poincaire, and Mandelbrot, and Taleb? Oh, no. We'd be in a world of trouble. Oh no.<br /><br />All the rest of us are complete idiots according to Taleb, from Plato on up to the present day. Oh, and that includes all thinkers, all agencies involved in trying to forecast future growth for society. Despite the fact that human civilization has progressed without Taleb's help for thousands of years, you get the distinct impression that he writes this off as just a fluke of nature, enormously unlikely good fortune on our behalf.<br /><br />He doesn't just condemn philosophers however. He condemns all of economics, all study of history, all of the life and physical sciences (except for physics), all of the humanities, and all of academics in general.<br /><br />If we would just listen to him, the messiah of the future, we'd all be so much better off. We'd see history more clearly, and we'd have much fatter bank accounts and more people at parties to intimidate with our lofty ideas.<br /><br />The thing that turned me off the most about this book is that the author seems to assume that every economist, politician, scientist and academician involved in trying to predict the future is completely blind to the fact that their predictions might be just a bunch of dog poop. They do know this. But they try anyway, to improve upon the process.<br /><br />AND, Taleb assumes that the common man or woman is a complete idiot that buys into these forecasts. We all know that the experts often don't know what they are talking about. That is, uh, common sense, is it not?<br /><br />I forced myself through this book to page 298, and took copious notes. The author said this was the end of his diatribe, so I stopped there (though the book does in fact continue to page 444).<br /><br />The greatest weakness I see in this book is that the author spends page after page condemning all this theorizing about how to predict the future and account for the unexpected, Black Swans, yet he fails to see that he is largely doing the same thing. He theorizes on and on, and offers very little in the way of solutions to implementing his ideas. He is what he condemns.<br /><br />The greatest strength in this book is that it very thoroughly outlines the key ways in which human beings/the human mind tricks itself into thinking that the future is predictable and the past is known. This is very important, fundamental knowledge. And I applaud the author for spelling it out for us. We need to know our limitations if we are to find a way past them.<br /><br />But if you make it to the end of this book, odds are you are going to have a fairly negative opinion of this man as being something like a child in a school yard screaming out his ideas to everyone, with no one listening, telling everyone how great he is and why you should all listen to him.<br /><br />Its very hard to take that sort of thing seriously, even if the temper tantrum has some serious basis in fact.<br /><br />Taleb needs to go talk to his fictitious author of renown, Yevgenia Krasnova, and ask her for some advice on how to write a book where you don't offend your reader on nearly every page.<br /><br />Otherwise, regardless of the value of his ideas, the thing is of value only to stoke thecampfire at night and roast some marshmallows.

0910316252|5.0|This is written to be readable by non-physicists and explains all technical points very clearly and with very little mathematics. However, it is also very complete, detailed and well balanced. It relates the development of quantum physics starting with Plank's black body radiation law. It is not until two thirds of the way through the book that it actually discusses the debates between Bore and Einstein, and that is as it should be, because that only makes sense in the context of what they and others had contributed and thought before the full theory was formulated. It is important, for instance, that it was Einstein who introduced probability into quantum physics in the first place (so that was not his main objection to the Copenhagen Interpretation).<br />It relates that Bore pointed out to Heisenberg that the uncertainty principle is a general property of waves, and therefore easy to see from Schrodinger's point of view. This has been overlooked in other books I have read. Here it is explained with simple diagrams, without assuming the knowledge of Fourier transforms that the physicists of that time presumably had.<br /><br />Though Einstein was completely wrong in doubting qm, his thought experiments were valuable. The one where Einstein forgot to take general relativity into account, as this book points out, served to show that macroscopic objects obey the rules of qm. This had not been clear in Bore's ideas. It is still useful; for example, it tends to rule out Roger Penrose's idea that gravity washes out qm effects when a body reaches a certain weight.<br />The EPR one really did show a fatal flaw in the Copenhagen Interpretation, though not in qm itself.<br /><br />The book fails to warn us early on that both Bore and Einstein were wrong in their own ways, each trying to retain something of classical physics. (It seems to me that the one who was closest to right was Schrodinger.)<br />This book seems to me to be already somewhat outdated. When the author says \\"reality\\", he, as in older books, means classical reality, and he tends to equate qm with Copenhagen some of the time. Schrodinger and Everett saw reality as being the state vector or wave function, and they realized that macroscopic objects can exist in quantum superpositions. They realized that whatever one says about quantum mechanics, classical physics is no more than an approxiation to it. This latter view has become dominant in recent years. He does mention the Many Worlds Interpretation at the end, but since the book is largely for people who are not already familiar with such views, they should be presented early on, or even used as a standard by which Bore's and Einstein's positions are judged.<br />With this one reservation, it is an excellent book.<br /><br />I add one more point: All these books say that the Copenhagen Interpretation was dominant until recent decades. However, from the time I first read about qm in the early 1950s in Fred Hoyl's ''Frontiers of Astronomy'' through 20 years of intensively studying and using qm (and taking one philosophy of science class) to converting a scattering formula from Messiah and other qm books to electromagnetic waves to solve a radar problem, I never heard the idea that there is a physical process called wave function collapse. Everyone assumed that qm is just the way the world is and that is all the interpretation one needs, the probabilities must come out of the mathematics in one way or another. I only encountered wave function collapse when I started editing and reading Wikipedia. I encountered Many Worlds when I then read Everett's article. So Schrodinger's idea that the wave function is reality has been much more common than these books say. The reason the interpretation is not often discussed is that it is simply that classical physics is only an approximation.  What philosophy is supposed to teach us is how to ask the right question.  The question is not how does quantum mechanics lead to probabilities; it is how do classical approximations lead to probabilities.; it is how do classical approximations lead to probabilities.

The stories, told in the first person by different characters, recount how each person encountered zombies and how they survived. The author has done a really good job of creating a hugely diverse group of characters ranging from doctors and soldiers to kids, politicians, and grifters.  Some of them survived by dumb luck, others by smarts or by another person's sacrifice. The stories rang true and many were really affecting, such as soldiers who were the victims of bad military decisions or ordinary people who didn't know what to do and did something random that worked but sometimes with hard consequences. Since each chapter is another story, this would be a great beach book or a book for people who have to read in quick snatches in a busy life. I truly think this is the best of the whole zombie genre and recommend it both to zombie fans and to those who are just curious about the whole thing.

0765365278|5.0|This happens to be the first Brandon Sanderson novel I actually read. I bought a copy of the hardcover of Elantris when it first came out and a copy of the paperback of Mistborn, the first in the Final Empire trilogy (which is now a continuing series) but somehow never got into them. I have a habit of reading a chapter or two of every new book that arrives before deciding mentally when to read it. In this case, I happened to buy the ebook of The Way Of Kings first along with a number of other ebooks for my Kindle. I enjoyed the first few chapters so much I immediately ordered the paperback. Now that I've finished breaking the spine of this massive 1250 page paperback, I wonder if I should have ordered the hardcover instead! If I'm taking a long time to get to the point, it's only to make the point that Brandon Sanderson does NOT take time to get to the point. Few epic fantasies are so terrific straight out the starting gate. I can think of several that take at least the entire first volume to get started then end on a cliff-hanger.<br /><br />The Way of Kings is not such a book. It's a massively detailed, exhaustively plotted, immensely satisfying volume. While obviously not complete in itself, it does present enough story to satisfy even the most demanding reader with enough spectacular story-telling, world-building, character development, and all-round terrific epic narration to fill a trilogy by other authors. The quality of writing, editing, book designing, publishing is so high, it's quite amazing that both the publisher and author are so prolific and yet so good. The closest example I can think of where an author and publisher took a bold plunge to produce an equally ambitious fantasy series of this high standard is Steven Erikson's Malazan Book of the Fallen series, which has not just concluded on schedule but has spawned an equally brilliant spin-off series in collaboration with Ian C. Essslemont, published by Bantam in the UK. In my opinion, Sanderson's WoK (his acronym, not mine, and one that cleverly reminds one of another similar acronym, WoT, which is the other great epic fantasy series he is also writing) rivals and in some ways already aims higher than the Malazan series, even though it's just one book down. That's because Sanderson's approach delves deeper into characters, making them more empathetic, their story arcs more identifiable. And also because his world and magic system is so brilliantly developed to the last detail.<br /><br />WoK is a brilliant epic fantasy by any standards. Coming from an author who is juggling at least one other epic fantasy series of his own (the Final Empire), an YA series (Alcatraz) and the final volumes of the Wheel of Time, to be followed presumably by the last two books in the prequel trilogy as well, this is a superb production. 0385344228|5.0|Defending Jacob is a well written legal thriller that goes beyond the normal novel in this genre into question of family and the relationship between parents and children.<br /><br />The backbone of suspense in Defending Jacob follows that of the standard legal thriller. There is the crime, the trial and then the end of the trial. Like a three act morality play. However, in Defending Jacob we know early on who committed the crime. The only question is whether the author, William Landay, will pull a plot rabbit out of his hat. But this is not that kind of book. Instead of innocence or guilt being revealed, what we have is the unfolding psychology of Jacob, a teenager accused of murdering a classmate. Much of the suspense comes from the complex portrait of the parents, who must live through the nightmare of the murder trail and their deeper understand of Jacob.<br /><br />The main character in the novel is Jacob's father, Andy, who was an Assistant District Attorney until Jacob was charged with murder. Andy's motivations and the complexity of his character are well drawn. The mechanics of the criminal justice system and Andy's clear eyed view of its failings is also fascinating (we can assume that this is the view of the author, who was an ADA). Andy has a self-absorption that is characteristically male and the portrayal of this is unusual for a male writer (it can be hard to see our own entitlement as men).<br /><br />This is not a classic suspense novel, since it's pretty clear where the novel is going. But what drives the plot and the suspense is the way the adults in the novel react. I read it in two days and it kept me up late the last night.<br /><br />The only parts of the novel that I skipped over where some of the details of the trail. These are probably more fascinating to a criminal attorney than they are to me. The reality of criminal trials is not nearly as fascinating as the portrayal in novels. The ADA tends to drive their point home, again and again, since they have experience with juries coming to surprising and unjustified conclusions. Compared to this, the trial was greatly summarized. But if it had been summarized even more, the plot would not have suffered.ince they have experience with juries coming to surprising and unjustified conclusions. Compared to this, the trial was greatly summarized. But if it had been summarized even more, the plot would not have suffered.

If you want to hear the theme music you associate with the trilogy of Batman movies - buy the Dark Knight (second movie) and you will love it. This one is not worth the price, save one track. Also this album does not provide a lot of music from the movie that was left out for reasons known only to the producer(s).

0143119680|3.0|I'm a sucker for books about lost manuscripts. I'm obviously also a sucker for books about doomed relationships involving magical creatures that somehow have happy ever afters, so it kinda a given that one day I'd break down and spend the big bucks to buy the book everyone has been talking about for the last six months.<br /><br />Real life historian with a focus on Alchemists through history, Deborah Harkness obviously is writing about what she knows, when she is weaving history, alchemy, and famous English libraries into her book. Where the novel falls short is its familiar treatment of familiar settings and couplings of other famous Paranormal Romance and Y/A books that have gone before it.  A Discovery of Witches is being hailed as Twilight for adults.  I'd like to throw Harry Potter in the mix as well. The upside is the literate aspects of the book, (here-in called ADOW for ease of typing's sake) which discusses Evolution, famous historical characters, and surprisingly pits Alchemy against Darwinism rather than the usual religion vs. evolution war, and for that I thought it was very unique. The downside is, she didn't stretch her creative wings enough but instead chose to ride on those, others had flown with before.<br /><br />This author is a real, honest to god historian. She has so much to play with, I don't know why she had to create a world so familiar, but clearly it is just what the market wanted. In the past she had two books published (probably through her college) that received a total of 5 book reviews. The books were well received critically, by her fellow historians but nobody else bought them. And then she wrote ADOW, a long winded Paranormal Romance and became famous.<br /><br />ADOW has about 1100 book reviews on Amazon. It has been written about in everything from People Magazine to the New York Times, and is going to become a movie. The reviews seem to fall into two camps; those who are madly in love with it, and those who absolutely hate it. I disagree with both. I thought it was at times brilliant and at times a massive disappointment. The disappointment is not for what it is so much as what it could be, and isn't. She is an excellent writer, she could have come up with something more creative then the following scenario, upon which much of the bones of the story hinges upon.<br /><br />1.A witch who was orphaned at a young age, whose parents were murdered by a powerful wizard who was threatened by their power. They sacrificed their lives to protect the protaganist and she has been raised by her Aunt. The parents do something with Magic to protect her before they die. She may turn out to be the most powerful wizard (oops sorry...) I mean Witch, ever.<br /><br />2.A much older male Vampire who meets her at school, is obsessed with a female who (while not a human like in Twilight) is still much more frail then he is and so he must always be on his guard with her. He knows he should stay away from her but he breaks in her house to watch her sleep. He loves the way she smells, and catalogs all the different flowers and herbs she smells like to him. He is at first angry and irritable with her, but he is massively protective and will kill anyone who threatens her. His Vampire friends are upset that he is attracted to this woman. Their love can't be consummated because he is afraid he might hurt her, or he wants to wait or something. I don't know, I got so tired of watching two adults act like teenage kids I can't remember if they ever actually had sex or not.<br /><br />J.K Rowling and Stephanie Meyers should ask for a portion of the receipts.<br /><br />Harkness's world is one of Humans, Witches, Vampires, and lastly the poor Daemon's (who aren't demons, but are basically slightly wacked out hyper creative sorts who are the Mozart's of the world, and often can't function in the real world. Other then visions of the future, the Daemon's kinda get short shrift as far as magical abilities goes).<br /><br />Theprotaganist, Diana Bishop is an American historian who has returned to Oxford, England to continue her studies in famous historical Alchemist's for a dissertation she is going to give. (The above is all applicable to Harkness as well, in fact there are so many similarities between the protagonist and the author, Diana's name could well have been Mary Sue.)<br /><br />Dr. Diana Bishop isn't just an intellectual with a penchant for studying historical Alchemy, she is also a witch from a long line of witches (going back of course to Salem's witches which all fictional witches seem to need to use as a touch stone.) For some inexplicable reason Diana \\"usually\\" refuses to use her magic, allotting herself only the occasional magical sin like fixing the washing machine when it goes on the fritz. While in the library she asks for a book called Ashmole 782, which it turns out has been hiding in the Bodleian Library for over a hundred years. Her magic rears it's head and breaks the spell, sending out a shock wave to the magical community.<br /><br />Suddenly the library is full of \\"Creatures,\\" all staring at her while she is trying to study. Vampires, Witches, and Daemon's follow her around waiting for her to call the book back up. They all want to get their grubby little paws on Ashmole 782, each group for different reasons. When Diana returns it, everyone wants her to call it back up and give it to them. The Witches want the book because it will tell them how to attack and kill Vampires magically. The Vampires want the book to destroy it for the above reason. And the Daemon's want the book because they hope it will explain how they came to be created. The poor Daemon's are the only ones who deserve the damn book in my opinion. The Witches are born so they know how they are created, Vampires are made, but no one knows what makes a Daemon, and so children who are born to human's are often treated like freaks by their terrified parents when the \\"Gifts\\" arrive. This one book is enough to nearly create a war between the species.<br /><br />Diana is approached by  the 1500 year old (and of course gorgeous) Vampire, Matthew Clairmont, who is also (of course) a brilliant academic with doctorate degrees in four different discipline . He is rich, aloof, distant towards women, and suddenly obsessed with average looking and socially awkward  Bella...I mean Diana. Can you tell this is a fantasy written by a middle aged academic?<br /><br />Vampire, Matthew Clairmont has wanted Ashmole 782 for over 100 years as he is trying to scientifically analyze what creates magical creatures. He is a smarter version of Edward who continues to go, not to highschool, but to one of the highest levels of college, and has studied with and learned from the brightest minds in the world. He hears about Diana and goes to the library where he sees her use her magic, and begins to basically stalk her to find out more about the book. These two break every magical rule in the book when they fall in love and team up to try to find the book again. Only instead of trying to find the book again, they basically run and hide.<br /><br />ADOW's settings are gorgeous, Oxford England, a Mansion in France, a magical Farmhouse in the midwest of America.  I also loved all the nods to historical people and places. At times lyrical and thought provoking it was also however maddeningly slow and in desperate need of editing. At over 600 pages ADOW has scene of meal after meal after meal. A surprising number meals given that one of the pair is a VAMPIRE.  Likewise, the main character, Diana is always fussing with her hair.  There are long (to me anyway)boring descriptions of the two of them taking Yoga together. Yoga. For god's sake. The two of them reading hand written copies of everything from Shakespeare to The Origin of Species, all signed to Matthew of course. And long winded descriptions of wine. What makes good wine, what the best wines are in the world today and by the way, Matthewhas them all and is willing to share. We get long descriptions of Diana being initiated into learning all about wine. You get the idea. He's catnip to a fictional historical academic, and the perfect fictional hero to a living one as well.<br /><br />I could not get a handle on Matthew's character. He is in the beginning oddly hostile and surprisingly angry at the slightest thing (again, shades of Edward), then he becomes shy and uncertain around her. He's 1500 years old, I doubt there would be much shyness left after having lived through the dark ages, and the black plague.<br /><br />Once these two lovebirds start exchanging lists of what the other smells like to them (he smells of cinnamon and clove and carnation pinks, she smells like...oh seriously who cares,) the plot almost dissolves. Instead of getting to the bottom of what is in the damn book (Ashmole 782) that has every creature's panties in a bunch for a 200 mile radius, these two go on Inter-species Yoga dates, and have long meals with lectures on wine tasting and long walks. Harkness by the way has a blog on wine tasting that has won awards so she is knowledgeable, perhaps too much so for a fictional escapist novel. Too much detail and something can lose its alure.<br /><br />At first Diana doesn't want anything to do with him. Then they start hanging out all the time because he wants information on the book, then they start to fall in love and he tells her they can never be together. She is crushed. The lovers almost kiss. She gets threatened by bad witches and he takes her to his Mother's castle in France to protect her and then leaves her, saying they can't be together at which point she turns into a watering witch and almost floods the Mansion with her tears and nearly drowns herself. In other words, just a week or two into knowing Edward, oops I mean Matthew, she is ready to die if she can't be with him. Where did my independent, brilliant, educated witch from the beginning of the book go?<br /><br />The forbidden and implied, doomed love affair of a Witch and a Vampire in ADOW is so all consuming to the story,  it loses some of the excitement a book like this needs to move the whole thing forward, and the obsessive focus on the romance takes away from any action or plot involving the magical book. Nothing much happens, and some of what does happen, happens off page. There are only, in over 600 pages, two times where I thought ....now we are talking, something serious is about to happen. Which again reminded me of Twilight.<br /><br />There are some fascinating themes, the idea of Vampires working in DNA labs to try to find out more about their origins and her idea of Daemon's was new to me. I thought it ridiculous however, that in all of history these are the only two who have crossed the bi-magical dating line. ADOW is at times fun and thought provoking. It is also at times as dull as watching the dust settle on the books our heroine loves so well, and that is the real tragedy here. It could be so much more.  But evidently, an intellectual Harry Potter meets Twilight is just what the public was clamoring for.he 1500 year old (and of course gorgeous) Vampire, Matthew Clairmont, who is also (of course) a brilliant academic with doctorate degrees in four different discipline . 0345522478|4.0|The first novel in an original, back-to-back three-book series The Iron Druid Chronicles--introducing a cool, new, funny urban fantasy hero<br /><br />Atticus O'Sullivan, last of the Druids, lives peacefully in Arizona, running an occult bookshop and shape-shifting in his spare time to hunt with his Irish wolfhound. His neighbors and customers think that this handsome, tattooed Irish dude is about twenty-one years old--when in actuality, he's twenty-one centuries old. Not to mention: He draws his power from the earth, possesses a sharp wit, and wields an even sharper magical sword known as Fragarach, the Answerer.<br /><br />Unfortunately, a very angry Celtic god wants that sword, and he's hounded Atticus for centuries. Now the determined deity has tracked him down, and Atticus will need all his power--plus the help of a seductive goddess of death, his vampire and werewolf team of attorneys, a sexy bartender possessed by a Hindu witch, and some good old-fashioned luck of the Irish--to kick some Celtic arse and deliver himself from evil.<br /><br />Sometimes it is really nice to read a fantasy written from a male perspective. There are some wonderful series out there where the MC is male; Jim Butcher's Dresden Files, Simon Green's Nightside series, Rob Thurman's Cal Leandros books (and yes, I know Rob is a woman).<br /><br />Sometimes, a series is like a relationship. It was so much fun in the beginning, there was a joy to the wild crazy world building and interactions as the players all got to know each other or as we began to know the players. However, all relationships over time change, and the books, and the characters need to change  as well. Obviously, if a character doesn't grow and change over a series of books, if they are NOT affected by the often horrific things they are subjected to by their creator, they  would become stagnant.<br /><br />However, by the same token, sometimes the books become so dark, grim, and morose we lose a much of the  joy we had when we first started reading the series. It doesn't mean we don't enjoy the author anymore,  or the character...we do. We're loyal fans,  just....we miss things the way they used to be, when every thing was fun and new and when our protagonist was so damn tortured. I love Dresden, but he's had such a wretched time he's a ghost now for God's sake.<br /><br />I picked up HOUNDED, within minutes settled in and went...\\"Yeah...now we are talking.\\" It was as if Hearne took the things we loved about so many of our favorite series, and then lightened everything back up. Who knows, maybe after 22 books (we should be so lucky), we'll all be worn out along with Atticus but as of now, he is a laid back hottie, who has a goddess or two in his pocket and after his bod, a stolen magical sword strapped to his back, and a telepathic dog who has a thing for French Poodles. It was smartly written, fun, and a delight, and it was kinda like falling in love again, with a brand new character. Count me in, I'm a new fan.<br /><br />A long time ago, hundreds of years ago, our hero Atticus, the last living druid on the planet, had the opportunity to steal a celtic god's magical sword. Aenghus &Oacute;g, has tried for centuries to get it back by having others attempt to do his dirty work, none of whom was able to pull off the stunt. Aenghus is still pissed about it and has decided to finally go after his sword in person.  Evidently Aenghus is a God of Love, but he is such a jerk, you are glad the magical sword is with the good guy, Atticus. This God has been hounding Atticus for hundreds of years and it looks like this time, he isn't going to stop until one of them is dead. Aenghus &Oacute;g isn't afraid to use the local police, Witches, and has even gone so far as to call in favors from other dieties to try to take Atticus down.<br /><br />Atticus may look too young to own his own Occult bookstore, (he looks like a 21 year old slacker), but he is over 21 hundred years old, and he didn't livethat long by  doing anything stupid, like picking fights with gods, but sometimes when the fight is brought to you, you gotta make a stand and utilize what resources you have, because the sword shouldn't be in the hands of a deity who is a bully. Atticus has a telepathic dog named Oberon who is his best friend, a team of lawyers who are kinda friends of his, one is a Werewolf and one is a Vampire...but that doesn't stop them from charging him $300.00 a billable hour. However, watching them in action...they're worth every penny. If I ever got in any kind of trouble, I'd want lawyers like these guys!<br /><br />Atticus is also friends with a little old Irish lady who likes to have a wee dram with him on the porch after he finishes burying the odd body or two in her back yard, which she is perfectly fine with as long as they are English. He has a deal with The Morrigan, a Goddess of Death who shows up either as a crow or a gorgeous naked lady, and a Coven of Polish Witches that may or may not be on his side. Also, there is a hot bartender who is more then she seems, then there is Brigid, and Flidais, and....seriously...just read the book. There are so many mythological characters coming and going out of his little store it is impossible to keep track. You don't feel lost when you're reading the books, you just kind of stand their with a silly grin on your face as something else awesome comes into play.<br /><br />I loved how Hearne weaves history and myth through the book, with Atticus having to make deals left and right if he wants to come out of this alive. It was great to read a new, strong, Urban Fantasy debut featuring a male protaganist who was smart and funny instead of just snarky and a smart ass. Atticus isn't out to save the world so much as he is out to save his ass, and if he has to do the right thing along the way, he will. It was really fun to have the magic AND the fun together again. I can't WAIT for the next book.times when the fight is brought to you, you gotta make a stand and utilize what resources you have, because the sword shouldn't be in the hands of a deity who is a bully. Information she has tried to hide and keep secret her entire life. The bad guys didn't tell her what she had to steal, so she only takes a penny (she even leaves one in its place), but the roar Dragos lets out once he finds it missing is so loud, glass breaks and buildings crack all across Manhattan.<br /><br />It is clear that he isn't going to see the humor in her theft, so Pia takes off to the Southern Demesnes of the Elves in an attempt to hide, and the chase is on. Dragos in his search for information about his thief comes across a video of her and once he does, everything changes. Oh he still wants to catch her, he just isn't so sure about what he's going to do once he does.<br /><br />The chemistry between the two was incendiary, the fights were spectacular, the affection and growing love was incredibly touching. Dragos and Pia have a lot of obstacles in their way. Dragos has pissed off the Elves by arriving in their lands to steal his thief, and the Dark Fae King Urien wants to kill both of them.<br /><br />Dragos is the equivalent of Wyr royalty, running one of the six Demesnes, a feudal system of dividing the lands. He has been giving orders and expecting them to be carried out for hundreds of years. He lives, works and owns a massive high-rise that is a modern day castle of industry with teams of security and lawyers and publicists, all of whom are stunned by his obsession and what some see an unnecessary distraction with Pia.<br /><br />Pia is a terrific character. She isn't powerful at all, is very vulnerable in this world she is inhabiting, however she is brave, loyal, and funny and doesn't do so good with the carrying out orders thing. She has to wade through some interesting Family/Royal politics to find her place in his world. She isn't sure she even wants to do it. A woman who was raised by her now dead mother, to hide who and what she is at all costs, keep her head low and be a loner, is now mated to the most powerful Wyr there is, and he does NOT like secrets. I have never read a Paranormal Romance that featured Pia's Shape shifter type and that surprise was great fun.<br /><br />There are a many common Paranormal Romance tropes going on in this story. The uber alpha, the group of warriors who all work for him, the chase of one lover for another (my only complaint is I half wished the chase lasted longer, it was so exciting!) On the surface, DRAGON BOUND doesn't sound that new or original but to me it stood out as one of the best I've ever read.<br /><br />It is going to be interesting to see how Harrison handles the next book in the series, STORMS HEART, which comes out August 2nd, featuring two of the characters we saw in DRAGON BOUND.<br /><br />Tricks the new Queen of the Dark Fae and former Publicist for Cuelebre Enterprises, and Tiego, one of Dragos inner circle and a ThunderBird are not twopeople who get along. At all. So I'm expecting some fireworks, or rather...thunder and lightning.<br /><br />I'm always a little concerned when the first book of the series starts with the Top Dog in the pecking order, or in this case top dragon. If you start at the top, where can it go from there? However I was delighted to find out who the next couple was because some of the best scenes involving side characters in DRAGON BOUND involved them.<br /><br />I hope Harrison can keep up the excitement and passion and originality that was in DRAGON BOUND, because if so, and I do not say this lightly, I feel this series could be on par with Kresley Coles Immortal's After Dark, and how exciting is that?...thunder and lightning. B0033H7VI6|4.0|It was my birthday and I had a gift certificate burning through my pocket, so I decided to check out some fixed blade knives for camping/ hiking/ general outdoors usage.  I've been really happy with my orange folding Endura for all of the finer cutting and slicing, but wanted something beefier for basic woodcraft and firebuilding.  I am by no means a wilderness or survival expert, but I grew up camping, hiking, and occasionally short backpacking trips.  Now my wife and I mostly camp in the local national forest system and enjoy less-than-rustic, mostly clear sites, so I am not constrained to weight (and this guy is a solid fellow).  I already have a Ka Bar I like, so I turned to the Becker line first.  I was on a pretty solid $70 budget max, so I could have covered the BK2 or the BK7 with a few extra bills and, originally, this was my plan.  I was bothered by the rather simplistic looking plastic grips, upgrading to Micarta was an extra expense I couldn't afford.  Then came the blade thickness.  I really had wanted a 0.25 inch thick blade, which the BK2 bears proudly, but unfortunately is shaped short and fat like a trowel to me.  People's only complaint about that knife seemed to be the heft to it, even moreso than the BK7.  The BK7 had the 7 inch blade size I liked better, but the thinner blade and was at the max of my price range.<br />Enter the SCHF9<br />It had the 1095 steel, the 0.25 blade width, and just shy of 7 inches (with 6 \\" of actual cutting surface).  I had a small Schrade folder from when I was a kid, but I haven't seen any since, so I thought I would test it out and if I hated it I could just exchange it towards one of the Beckers.  The knife arrived beautifully, protected well in it's box, even though some textbooks in my order crushed it.  The blade steel was clean and spotless, almost a bead blasted- gunmetal gray tone.  Heavy, as expected, with a beautiful curve to the blade that resembles the kopis style on my CS Spartan.  The blade was sharp enough to shave a small patch of hair on my arm, but still got even sharper with a few pulls through a carbide sharpener I have.  The Kraton handles don't line up perfectly with the tang, but are definitely close enough for a knife in this price range.  It does not effect the grip or functionality of the knife.  They have a comfortable swell to them and are \\"checkered\\" with a circle/ half circle pattern which gives it a nice, slightly rubbery grip.  I fastened a 550 cord wrist strap to the knife, and I can comfortably move from choking up on the blade with the extra notch in front of the handle and my thumb on the spine of the blade for more control, to a basic forward grip with my thumb on the jimping, to just my first two or three fingers on the end of the handle with the support of the strap for chopping.<br />Much has been said about the relative cheapness of the sheath.  Is it as good as a custom molded sheath?  Most probably not.  But is it functional as it stands?  I think so.  I haven't gone and hiked the Appalachians with it on my hip or anything, but the ballistic nylon- type material looks as if it would hold up for most average activity transporting the knife on the hip.  You can velcro the sheath onto your belt, which is nice if you hate taking your belt on and off for holsters and such.  There is a shoelace strength cord on the bottom for securing around your thigh, which I automatically replaced with more 550.  It sits nice on the hip, a little lower than the top of the belt so you can move around without it poking you in the side (if you have some extra in the middle like me, or wearing a puffy jacket, this would be a very nice feature).  One thing I was concerned about from the pictures was the retention strap and the plastic insert to protect the blade.  It looked to be velcro, but the strap does have a metal snap on it, which I liked.  Schrade must have read the complaints about the blade bouncing in the plastic protector as well, because the firsttime I attempted to seat the blade into it, the blade physically separated a small portion of plastic holding the sides together.  I had to shake some plastic slivers out after, but from then on, it felt fairly snug.  Still not tight enough to hold it in place if you turn it upside down, run, or jump with it, but just enough that I don't notice much rattling.  There is a little place on both sides of the blade surface that looks like the plastic rubs gently with the blade, but I take that as a tradeoff for not having it shaking around in there.  Nothing a little oil didn't fix right up.  There is a small pouch on the front of the knife for a whetstone or fire steel (or both, actually), but not really big enough for, say, a pistol mag if you had that in mind for any reason (unless its a 380 sized mag).  It could probably fit a multitool of some sort as well.<br />All and all, I am very happy with this purchase.  I looks promising enough that I am going to keep it and take it on the next camping trip to test it outdoors.  If you actually read all of this review, thank you.  It took me a lot of time looking around on Amazon to find something that fit my needs, and I was hoping I could help answer some questions that I originally had about it before I bought it.

1400052181|4.0|I've been wanting to read this book for a while, but came into it expected a Mary Roach-like pop science book. Which it is-- about every other chapter. (Though with less humor...) Skloot tells the story and history of the HeLa cells. And she recognizes that telling this story involves more than just a clinical recitation of the cells themselves and what occurs in the labs (though much of this is surprisingly dramatic). Skloot also tells the story of Henrietta Lacks-- her life, her death, and her legacy-- not just her lab legacy but the (often tragic) story of her family. Skloot walks a really difficult line here. I'm not convinced she always stays on the right side of it, but she does succeed in giving Henrietta Lacks' children a platform to share their own voices and experiences. This really feels like three books-- one on how racial inequality and race relations plays out on the micro-level (the Lacks' family has a \\"white side\\" and a \\"black side\\"-- this doesn't get a lot of attention in the book but still very interesting), one on the more institutional discrimination intrinsic in our society (medical care being the obvious topic here, not just Henrietta's but also her children and grandchildren, but also discussions of race and prison), and one on the science and politics of the cells themselves (I learned so much about intellectual property as it relates to our own bodies-- enough to make me concerned). Skloot does a decent job at weaving these together, but it still feels a bit rough, thus the 4 stars.<br /><br />Worthwhile read.

B000S0GYRU|4.0|The 1975 SUSPIRIA was so unlike most other horror films from its era that its acquired a near legendary reputation, and though its famous extremely violent murder scenes now seem almost comically faked, the film itself has retained its aura of being something very special even more than thirty years later. Part of what helped it is its extreme strangeness: the film takes place in a mysterious dance academy outside Freiburg, Germany where the teachers guard a dangerous secret and where several of the students keep dying in horrible circumstances. Dario Argento, who had made quite a name for himself by this time in Italy for his visually imaginative (but weakly plotted and characterized) giallo films, was helped enormously by a number of his collaborators for this film: his co-screenwriter Daria Nicolodi, who helped him craft a genuinely unusual storyline involving ancient witchcraft; his cinematographer Luciano Tovoli and production designer Giuseppi Basan, who crafted gorgeous dreamlike settings and lights (both mostly in saturated colors) in which to display the mayhem; the music group Goblin, who crafted the famously disturbing score with Argento; and Jessica Harper, his star who makes something genuine out of her completely underwritten part.<br /><br />Some of the sequences (such as one near the beginning involving Harper watching a young frightened woman run through the woods in a driving thunderstorm parallel to Harper's taxicab) are nearly unforgettable. As always with Argento's films, the whole storyline doesn't make much sense in the end (why are the witches even running a dance academy in the first place?), but this is one of the few times when his sophisticated visuals make you not even care.
